Free Talk

Random Notes on a Cool Morning 250

— Runiu

There are legends about Zhang Xianzhong everywhere in China, which shows that everyone considers him to be quite unique. I was also one of the people who considered him to be quite unique.

When I was a child, I saw a book called "Wushuang Pu" (Table of Peerless Heroes), which was written by people in the early Qing Dynasty. It took extremely special and unique figures in history, drew a portrait of each, and wrote some poems on one side, but there seemed to be no bad people. Therefore, I later thought that I could choose the most special figures in history, who actually represent a kind of Chinese character, and write a Chinese "History of People," like Carlyle's "Heroes and Hero Worship" in England, and Emerson's "Representative Men" in America. However, it must include both good and bad, there are Su Wu who endured hardships by eating snow, Xuanzang who sought the law at the risk of his life, and Zhuge Liang who "exhausted himself to the utmost, and died only after death," but there are also Wang Mang who stubbornly believed in ancient methods and "died only after death," and Wang Anshi who half seriously and half jokingly reformed the law; Zhang Xianzhong was certainly also included. But now I have no intention of writing it at all.

Books like "Shu Bi" (History of Sichuan) record Zhang Xianzhong's murders in great detail, but they are also scattered, making it seem as if he was like "art for art's sake," specializing in "murder for murder's sake." He actually had other purposes. He did not kill many people at first, he certainly wanted to be the emperor. Later, he knew that Li Zicheng had entered Beijing, followed by the Qing army entering the pass, and he was left with only the road to decline, so he began to kill, kill... He clearly felt that the world no longer had anything of his own, and now he was destroying other people's things. This is exactly the same as the mood of some emperors in the late dynasties,

who burned the books, antiques, and treasures collected by their ancestors or themselves before they died. He still had soldiers, but no antiques, so he just killed, killed, killed...

But he still had to maintain the army, which was actually just maintaining the killing. He killed so many people that there were no common people left, so he sent many of his more trusted subordinates to the soldiers to eavesdrop. If there was any complaint, they would jump out, arrest him, and execute his entire family (his soldiers seemed to have families, perhaps they were captured women). He used killing to govern the army, and used the army to kill. He himself was finished, but he wanted to achieve the end of mutual destruction. Don't we also not cherish other people's or public things very much?

So Zhang Xianzhong's actions, although seemingly bizarre at first glance, are actually very ordinary. What is bizarre is how those people who were killed would always stretch out their necks and wait for him to kill, and only when Prince Su of the Qing Dynasty came to shoot him to death, did they become slaves and be saved, and even say that this was predestined, which is the so-called "playing the flute without bamboo, an arrow pierces the chest."

But I think that this prophecy poem was made up by later generations, and we don't know what people really thought at that time.

War and Peace 251

— Yuan Fei

Japan has occupied the Eastern Provinces and is now expanding its military in a frenzied manner, preparing for the final decisive battle to divide China. The reason is said to be "for the peace of the Orient." At the same time, according to recent reports, Kato Kanju, Akita Ryosuke, and others have launched a "Friends of Far East Peace Association" to support the anti-war conference convened by the world's writers Barbusse and others in Shanghai in September.

In the end, is the peace said by Party A the same thing as the peace said by Party B?

Obviously, the imperialists mean that if you give me everything I want, there will be peace. Otherwise, I will have no choice but to take "self-defense measures." To put it plainly, the peace they speak of is equivalent to war. The reason why they mention "peace" is just to create a smokescreen. Everyone knows that a smokescreen is a kind of weapon. But there are also people who truly love peace and oppose war, that is, the working masses under the heavy pressure of life. They know from the cruel experience of real life who the war is for, and who really suffers in the war are themselves and their fellow workers. They oppose war, and oppose those who incite war for their own benefit. Of course, this kind of understanding will lead them to a more active struggle.

However, in today's newspaper, a gentleman published a brilliant opinion in an article discussing the "Japanese Agricultural and Industrial Revolution" (?!): saying that the Japanese working farmers can only be liberated in a world war, and that opposing war will

"lead to the ridiculous conclusion of opposing world war, that is, opposing the Japanese agricultural and industrial revolution."

How is it? Is this conclusion ridiculous or not?

However, this gentleman has even more brilliant insights: saying that the Japanese people's opposition to war "is just pretending (?), and hypocritically expressing sympathy for Chinese friends, and showing that they are unwilling to lag behind," advising the Chinese people not to be "deceived" by them. Of course, we do not deny that there are many people who verbally say they oppose war to deceive people, just as we do not deny that there are many people who create reasons to "deceive" people into participating in war. But the genuine anti-war movement launched by the Japanese working masses is unrelated to this. Just

according to the Japanese government's announcement, in the past year alone, more than 7,000 Japanese revolutionary activists who took anti-war as one of their central tasks were arrested. And we know that dozens of these literally died. The Japanese masses want to use their freedom, blood, and lives to deceive the Chinese people, that deception is really stupid. It is far less effective than our smart people, who only need to write black characters on white paper.

Who is for war, who is for peace, the facts can testify for us.

"Letting Off Steam"-ism 252

— Cao Juren

Among the very sparse audience, we watched Lubitsch's "Million Dollar Legs."

Some time ago, the Shanghai petty bourgeoisie had many answers to the question of "What would you do if you won 500,000 yuan?" But Lubitsch's ten answers to "If I Had a Million" were not liked by the Shanghai petty bourgeoisie, even though the aviation lottery was about to be drawn. Two of Lubitsch's answers were particularly profound: a prisoner about to be executed suddenly received a check for a million dollars; he walked to the execution ground, shouting "I am a millionaire." Then, three soldiers lightly used a check for a million dollars as payment for an old debt at a grocery store, watching the grocery store owner turn into a millionaire out of nowhere, while they stared blankly at the railing. The rest of those answers mostly wrote about the desires suppressed in the subconscious, which were greatly vented under the opportunity of a million-dollar check. A clerk in a porcelain shop, because of the compensation for breaking porcelain on weekdays, smashed the porcelain in the shop to his heart's content on this day; fashionable girls decorated their living rooms and bedrooms to their heart's content, turning the lights on and off; a small employee who had been feeling resentful for too long swaggered into the manager's office and scolded the manager before leaving; a couple who were upset that their new car had been hit bought eight cars and crashed them until they were all crashed. This is like Freud said, satisfying our desires in dreams; being oppressed by the economic system on weekdays, once we get the opportunity, we explode into a happy revenge, which is called "letting off steam"-ism.

Not only the chance of economic good luck, to make the suppressed desires erupt into a happy revenge; but the chance of power good luck will also play the same happy revenge. Usually hungry, we watch others eat steak and pigeon eggs outside the restaurant, swallowing our own taste; once we get rich, we must at least spit saliva on the waiter's face and eat the expensive dishes over and over again. People who are usually trampled on and insulted and dare not resist, once they have power in their hands, settle their grievances, someone is a high official in the court, someone is beheaded and exiled, stringing together a book of returning home in glory, giving the old friends a little color to see. The opposite of heroism seems to be "letting off steam"-ism. Since heroes are people who win the jackpot of the power lottery, the object of venting is of course our ordinary people. The power lottery must be constantly drawn, and the heroes who win the jackpot must be constantly produced. Our ordinary people are like the big and small porcelain in the porcelain shop, smashed batch after batch.

After letting off steam, then what? Soda water loses its fizz, leaving only a bowl of cold water; shochu loses its alcohol, leaving only a bowl of cold water. Porcelain shop clerks, fashionable girls, small employees, and couples, they all rented foreign buildings, bought cars, and hired many male and female servants, playing cards, and taking rides, and that's it. Will the heroes who win the power jackpot have other tricks?

So Lubitsch, he never dares to do a certain kind of experiment: either divide thousands of yuan to 10,000 people, or divide 10,000 yuan to a thousand people. Because if he did that, not only would the interweaving of tragedy, comedy, and tragicomedy be beyond the scope of several types, but it would also imply another major meaning.

Reading "An Outline of Literary Creation" 253

— Hanqi

A few days ago, I saw someone advocating for adaptations based on original works in "Free Talk," and I wholeheartedly agreed. Previously, there were popular versions adapted from literary masterpieces like "Destruction" and "The Iron Flood." Later, I heard people say that the popularity of "Revolutionary Literary Giant Gorky," compiled and translated by Tao Fen, was due to the compiler's accessible and popular writing style. Now, "An Outline of Literary Creation," compiled and written by Mr. Hua Di, who aims to make theory accessible, has also been published. The compiler tells us that this book is largely adapted from Hiroshi Kawaguchi's "An Outline of Emerging Literature."

First of all, we must admit that the work of adaptation itself contains the meaning of popularization. Because, at least reading adapted works or theories can save us from memorizing names like "Dostoevsky," which are far removed from our lives, and from looking at the rather laborious words "circumstance" and "emphasis," as well as long sentences and complex grammar "that may be revised." This is the benefit of adaptation in terms of "form." At the same time, another main reason for adaptation is, taking "An Outline of Emerging Literature" as an example, it is certainly a good book in general, but if the original were selected and translated as is, it would cite many unfamiliar Japanese works, which would not only be incomprehensible to readers but also genuinely "uninteresting." Therefore, as long as the adapter has a deep understanding of the original text and replaces it with precise examples that do not conflict with the principles of the original, '...in China, such as Lu Xun's "The True Story of Ah Q"; and the recently published Mao Dun's "Midnight," etc.... can also be called a large-scale novel.' Like this sentence, readers who have read the book can understand it at a glance, and those who have not read it will not feel unfamiliar because the examples cited are "Chinese." This citation will further ignite the desire to read "the novels in the examples," and a sense of intimacy will arise spontaneously. Therefore, I acknowledge that Mr. Hua Di's adaptation work is extremely meaningful.

When reading translated books on literary theory, even if I try my best to follow the context word by word, I sometimes still feel "strained." Saying this, I dare not have the slightest disrespect for the translator of that book. For example, when I recently finished reading the article "Plekhanov's Criticism" with great "effort" and felt "refreshed," my first thought was: "If someone could adapt this article... its impact would probably be greater, and there would probably be more readers."

I remember Mr. Lu Xun admitting in "On Translation" that there are different levels of readers. I think, based on experience, advanced readers are the translators, intermediate readers are those who can fully understand the translation with effort, and low-level, that is, the majority of readers, are those who cannot understand or have difficulty understanding the translation, but who are hungrily in need of "spiritual food." If this classification is roughly correct, then here, we can see the great significance of "adaptation" work.

Before discussing "how to adapt," we must first admit that adaptation is an extremely difficult task. To adapt a work or theory in a way that is "deep and easy to understand," the first thing is to completely and thoroughly "understand" the content of the original book, and then talk about how to express it simply and accurately in one's own plain language. Honestly speaking, I dare not ask for advice from some of the recent book compilers (not to mention those who copy). Cutting out a certain translated passage and adding "Plekhanov said:" above it, or cutting out a section of a work and adding a few sentences of "concept" above it: "This expresses the consciousness of the petty bourgeoisie," without fully "grasping" the "positiveness of the theme," so-and-so's work "lacks understanding of materialistic dialectics," he must "learn" dialectics, "fight for the method of dialectics"... Such "adaptation" is not only a waste, but simply "lazy." In technical terms, it only proves that this person has "no understanding" and "no digestion" of the original theory. Even if an "author" wants to prostrate himself, he cannot accept this critic's "advice." "Learn," yes, how to learn? Honestly speaking, "learning" from someone who himself has "no understanding" and cannot "inspire respect" is really a bit "cannot help but speak out." It seems that that tone and that manner are also a bit difficult to "convince" people.

I've digressed; let's get back on track. Finally, I hope that adapters with this kind of "conceptual" and "preachy" approach will not continue to "develop" their faults, although I dare not "emphasize" their "learning." If, during these difficult years, more popular versions of works and theories that have been painstakingly adapted can appear, I will be their loyal reader and supporter.

Buzzing Sounds 254

— Nanjiao

On a sweltering summer night, while listening to friends chatting, the buzzing of mosquitoes disturbs the surroundings, making one feel depressed and feel that these days are not going well.

To extend this feeling, although there are few disturbances from the buzzing of mosquitoes during the day, when one occasionally takes a moment to open the newspaper, where is there not some buzzing sound that makes one feel uncomfortable after reading it?

Starting from the world, the World Economic Conference is ringing the death knell amidst this buzzing sound. Originally, the words of diplomats and politicians were so loud, but when they gathered together, they were like the buzzing of mosquitoes. Just look at from the Washington Conference to this World Economic Conference, has there ever been a time when a sentence was said that made the world feel happy?

Looking back at the situation in China, this country is even more pitiful. The warlords armed millions of homeless people without clothes or food, aiming at each other, but none fired the first shot; the bureaucrats sharpened their heads to earn people's wealth, silently embracing women or opium pipes to live their days.

Then there are the urban monks buried in the cities, who have all kinds of tragic events of fighting for love and jealousy, and hacking and shooting every day; in another corner, the victims of disasters are selling their wives and children and eating raw meat and blood, reflecting how obvious the contradictory phenomena of this social life, this dying period of capitalism, are, and how uncomfortable this state of neither life nor death is!

In the contradictory social life of the dying period of capitalism, the war drums for justice were silent for a while, and the buzzing sounds and the蝇营狗苟活响 everywhere. People who felt uncomfortable in life also abandoned social justice, sought stimulation, and went astray; the darkness in the political arena and the fraud in the market stirred up people throughout the whole society, making a never-ending commotion. This is a dark picture covered by buzzing sounds.

Grain Prices for Dongxin Glutinous Rice Continue to Drop Slightly by Two Cents 269

(South Shanghai) Yesterday, over thirty sources of supply arrived. Both retail and wholesale demand remained good. Although the situation was slightly tight, the market remained stable. Prices for thick glutinous rice such as Changbang Su, Tongbang Nangang, and thin rice such as Qingjiao Songjiang continued to rise for higher grades. Medium and lower grades remained unchanged. Duyang Jian rice, due to a shortage of goods, showed a slight increase. Ordinary rice remained flat and weak. The overall market for Northern Jiangsu packaged rice remained just over a thousand packages. Prices for factory-fresh Dongxin glutinous rice turned soft again, with prices reduced by two cents. Thin rice arrivals were small and sales were flat, with prices showing a slightly soft trend. Early rice also showed a weak trend. Dujian rice prices dropped slightly by one cent. Although the amount of Yangbu Jian rice arriving was not large, demand was sluggish and prices continued to decline. White Yuan rice was stable. Shipments of Dongxin glutinous rice increased, causing prices to drop by two cents.

In the future, there might be some opportunities for coordination and application. Yesterday, the market was active, but prices were stable. Business Management (Twenty-Five)

A Glimpse of Seafood Production Areas by Cao Wenhai 278

Our country has thousands of miles of coastline, encompassing cold, temperate, and tropical zones. Yet, we still rely heavily on imports for seafood, incurring significant annual losses. This is alarming. The low production is due to poor fishing practices. Fishermen lack knowledge and adhere to outdated methods, making them unable to compete with scientific advancements. The quantity of catches and the distance of voyages are not properly calculated, making their inefficiency clear. Furthermore, while other countries have organized and well-funded fishing fleets, our investors are unwilling to invest, and those who could organize are unwilling to do so. Coastal areas are plagued by banditry, robbery, and kidnapping. The government fails to protect, and the people fail to advance. How can we avoid being outcompeted by foreign fishermen? The variety of seafood is vast. What I will discuss here is a general overview of their production areas.

(1) Shark fins are produced in Fujian, China, and abroad in Japan and the islands of Southeast Asia. (2) Sea cucumbers are produced in Vladivostok, Japan, and the islands of Southeast Asia. (3) Dried scallops are produced in Yantai, China, and abroad in Japan. (4) Abalone is mostly from Japan, followed by the United States. Guangdong, China, has a small production. (5) Squid is produced in Guangdong, Fujian, and coastal provinces. Foreign countries such as Japan, the United States, and Russia also produce large quantities. (6) Dried shrimp is produced in Jiangsu, Zhejiang, and Yantai, but imports from Japan and the islands of Southeast Asia are more common. (7) Shrimp roe is produced in Jiangbei, Jiangsu Province, with little production elsewhere. (8) Mussels from Ningbo, Zhejiang Province, are of good quality, but the market is dominated by Japanese goods. (9) Laver seaweed is produced in Ningbo, Zhejiang, but Japanese imports dominate the market. (10) Tribute dried goods are produced in Ningbo and Yantai, China. (11) Cuttlefish are unique to China and produced in Ningbo and Yantai. (12) Fish skin is produced in Xiamen, Fujian, and in Japan and the islands of Southeast Asia. (13) Fish maw is produced in Ningbo and Yantai, China, and in San Francisco and the islands of Southeast Asia. (14) Cuttlefish (dried cuttlefish) is produced in Daishan, Zhoushan, Shengsi, Sijiao, Liuheng in Zhejiang Province, and in Yantai in Shandong Province, and Liansi in Jiangsu Province. The annual production volume ranges from 20,000 to 30,000 dan, with a minimum of 10,000 dan. The main sales regions are Sichuan, Hunan, Jiangxi, and Fujian. Local demand in Shanghai is limited. The largest production comes from Yantai in Shandong Province. Japan is the only other country that produces this product. (15) Kelp is only produced in Japan and Russia. (16) Sea swallows are produced in Ningbo and Wenzhou, as well as in Japan. (17) Clam dried goods are produced in Yantai, Shandong, and in Japan. (18) Jellyfish skin is unique to China and produced in Ningbo and Fujian. (19) Red algae is only produced in Japan. (20) Seaweed is only produced in the islands of Southeast Asia.

Essays

Eyes Open, Mouth Closed 279

— Pei Yu

"Eyes Open, Mouth Closed" is a thoroughly authentic colloquial expression, unlike "Eyes Open and Closed," which doesn't quite fit as a common saying. Don't underestimate it; though only four characters, it holds numerous clever strategies, worthy of being tucked away in Zhuge Liang's or Qing Tian's brocade pouch. It's a secret to navigating the world, a subtle method for dealing with people, and can be considered a "golden rule" or "inviolable principle." See the annotation below:

A renowned British headmaster, recently deceased, once imparted this wisdom to his students: "If you wish to enjoy the pleasures of life, then your eyes must be wide open, and your mouth must be tightly shut." Now, following the ancient practice of shortening phrases, we condense it to "Eyes Open, Mouth Closed." "Eyes Open" or "Your eyes must be wide open" doesn't mean using them to fend off beggars or confront extortionists; it means using them to discern people, recognize things, and observe expressions. Evidence follows:

A British expatriate living in Italy adhered to the first half of this esteemed headmaster's instruction, leading to a valuable invention. During a holiday, he and a few friends went hiking in the mountains, an area known for its marble. As he wandered through the mountains, his eyes wide open, he carefully observed his surroundings. His gaze happened to fall on a small patch of rock that appeared to be a pale red color. He approached, picked up the stone, and examined it closely, scrutinizing it repeatedly. The more he looked, the more he admired it, delighting him so much that he began to dance with joy, leaving his friends utterly bewildered. What he held was a piece of exceptionally beautiful, pale red marble. As a result, he opened a quarry there, exclusively mining this rare marble, yielding a substantial quantity.

As for the esteemed headmaster's second half of the instruction, it isn't entirely novel. Our ancestors had already articulated similar maxims. While the wording may not be groundbreaking, the meaning is profoundly insightful. "Your mouth must be tightly shut" certainly doesn't mean clamping your lips, clenching your teeth, refusing to eat or drink, and becoming a "mute monkey." It undoubtedly teaches the importance of "speaking cautiously" and aligns with the idea of "eating more and talking less." In these chaotic times, when right and wrong are blurred, speaking carelessly could land you in a terrifying situation. Therefore, it is crucial to keep your mouth tightly shut.

In closing, here are four nonsensical, incongruous, and modern verses: Eyes wide open, never fear being unprosperous in this life; mouth tightly shut, no need for life insurance!

Wives and Sons 280

— Hua Ling

"Other people's wives are better, one's own sons are better." If this isn't entirely wrong, then, aren't we Chinese, with our eyes fixed solely on the "foreign" word, destined to be cursed with only "better wives" and "better sons" from other people?

"Mountain songs" and "folk tunes"—don't we Chinese have "Five Watches Tune," "Eighteen Touches," "Little Nun," and other widely circulated melodies? More fashionable ones like "Drizzling Rain" and "Three Butterflies," or even more "suitable for refined ears" ones like "Tears on the Barren Mountain" and "Crying for My Wife"? Some are "vulgar without being offensive," others "sorrowful without resentment"—how wonderful! But alas, "Dearest," "Darling," are said to be no match for the Western classic "I Love You."

Even in music, the "erhu" is for fortune-telling and storytelling, the "huqin" is what beggars play for money, and "sheng, xiao, guan, and di" flutes are only for sending off monks, spirits, and conducting rituals. So, even though it's a tune for the "dressing table" or "mourning the dead," when played with Western drums and trumpets, everyone finds it "meaningful." Holding a "violin" case, even if it's empty, is considered "modern" and commands respect. When it comes to food and drink, foreign things are even better. Even if it's meat from the same pig, if it's cooked into "four happiness meatballs" or "steamed pork with rice flour," it feels pointless. But if it's cooked half-raw and placed on a "plate"—aha! "Pork chops, foreigners eat that." Similarly, "wine" brewed from wheat with a high "alcohol" content—"brandy," "beer," "cocktail"—is so noble! "Yuanliang" and "gaoliang" spirits are only for rickshaw pullers and Russian beggars to drink. As for "wheat spirits," it's impossible not to add the word "stinky."

In conversation, one simply must sprinkle in a few phrases of "pidgin English"; in writing, one must sprinkle in a few unnecessary English words. This demonstrates the "speaker's" or "writer's" level of sophistication, proving they've at least nibbled on the "edge of bread" and are worthy of being called a "high-class Chinese." If one can replace the "robe and gown" with "foreign clothes," that's even more of a "thumbs up."

In short, everything is "better from other people," nothing is "better from oneself." As long as the "wife" doesn't have a "son," that's truly terrible.

Memories 281

— Fu Yizhi

Though my head was covered with a bamboo hat, my torso and waist were draped in a short shirt and shorts made of rough cloth, my skin, unaccustomed to hardship, ultimately couldn't withstand the relentless, penetrating sunlight and began to peel.

Though my bare feet weren't submerged in the three or four inches of mud, the parts above—knees, calves, shins, and fibulas—were constantly kissed by the scorching sun. My fair skin gradually transformed into a coarse yellow.

Though I knew that my entire hide would soon become as impervious to the sun as Old Wang's rough skin, resembling black cowhide, the heat and stinging sensation was almost unbearable for this child who had just left school and was now practicing in the fields. Moreover, the sharp awns of the rice plants, symbolizing swords, continuously slashed at my shins and fibulas.

The sun was so intense, the sky so clear, that this weather persisted for almost half a month. The soil gradually hardened from being waterlogged. The rice seedlings in the fields turned from lush green to withered yellow. Naturally, I had to follow my eldest, second, and third elder brothers, driving the dragon bone waterwheel day and night, constantly sucking up small amounts of clear water from the distant streams or springs and laboriously leading it to our own cultivated fields to irrigate the soil and save the wilting rice seedlings.

The water in the springs and streams wasn't inexhaustible. Once they dried up, we had to wait for them to slowly refill. Sometimes, when everything else in the world was asleep, we would sit or lie by the stream, pond, or field path, waiting for water.

When the rice was harvested, dried, and winnowed, the landlords would unceremoniously take away most of what we had harvested, leaving us with only enough to sustain us for two months. "Warm in winter, yet children cry from cold; abundant harvest, yet wives weep from hunger"—this wasn't unusual for us tenant farmers.

Though I am now living a wandering life, I shudder to recall the time I had to suspend my studies and farm at home. I dare not echo the high-sounding rhetoric of writers sitting in tall buildings with electric fans, advocating "going to the countryside," because the countryside is deteriorating year after year, something they in Shanghai, the Paris of the East, cannot imagine!

Qingdao Poems 282

— Yang Yao

Qingdao Seaside

A road of poetic and artistic scenery stretches,

Silk whips, hat shadows, and carriages fragrant.

The waves care not for human affairs,

But busily carry guests away, surging and flowing.

The domineering aura of Jiaodong is silently extinguished,

Heroes washed away by the waves.

Heaven leaves behind the picturesque青山,

Forever accompanying the poet in solitude.

Visiting Laoshan

A giant rock blocks the sky, seemingly a dead end,

A path reappears where the spring flow is interrupted.

The mountain peaks seem to smile and compete to greet guests,

I love the 青山 and cannot bear to leave.

The rugged and treacherous path is hard to walk,

The nine wonders of the water are all clear.

I want to move my home to live in the valley,

Morning and evening, washing my ears and listening to the sound of the springs.

Night at a Seaside Residence

Jagged rocks clash with the tide daily,

Seemingly crying out against injustice.

The angry waves fill the sky and silence everything,

The heavenly wind roars like a dragon every night.

Morning at a Seaside Residence

After the snow, the sky opens up clear blue,

Sails and masts bring the dawn brightness.

Ten thousand mountains have just emerged from their bath,

Washing away the sleepiness with the morning.

The Fourth Platoon Leader of the Northeast 283

— Zhang Henshui

Chapter 12

A Poor Man Joins the Army, A Sick Man Falls Behind, A Sacrifice Willingly Discharged, A Tenderhearted Soldier Makes a Promise. It was probably about two hours before Jiang returned. Gui then complained, "You're not seventy or eighty years old, why does talking to you go on and on without end?" Jiang said, "It's not that I like to talk, but listening to what he said, it's so pitiful, so we just kept talking. Usually, when we see soldiers, we're already unhappy in our hearts. But according to the soldiers themselves, their plight is simply inhuman. In the future, when you see soldiers, you should pity them more." Gui Zhi said, "You're talking in such a roundabout way, what are you talking about anyway? I don't understand." Jiang thought for a while and smiled, saying, "I have a bellyful of things to say, I can't hold it in, so I said it as soon as I saw you. Guess what?" Alas! Before she had said what was on her mind, she sighed in such a pathetic way. Gui Zhi knew that her mother was a softhearted person, so it must have been that person who said some bitter things, which aroused her mother's compassion. If she just kept asking her mother, the story would definitely be even longer, so she lowered her head and stopped talking to her mother. That evening, Jiang went to the back courtyard again to see the sick man, and also found a pair of old cotton socks in the box and gave them to Sheng Shimin, because they were men's socks and were just lying in the box unused. Today, they ran into the Zhao family cooking mutton dumplings, and they insisted that Jiang eat there, and also called Gui Zhi to go. Sheng Shimin had rested in the warm room for half a day, and his spirits were much better. Soldiers don't get to see meat once a month, and when they saw dumplings, their mouths watered. Zhao also let him half-lie and half-sit on the rattan chair and eat two bowls of dumplings. After he had filled his stomach, his illness was even more reduced. Having nothing to do, he talked to everyone about the hardships of the army. After he had talked for two or three hours, not only did he talk about how Jiang and her daughter felt sorry for the soldiers, but also Zhao's idea of letting his son join the army to protect the country became somewhat cold. (145)

Wonders of the World 284

— Dong Yingdai

Who lost an airplane? This is a really strange thing. In Muncie, Indiana, an airplane was found in a field, and they are searching for the owner. A farmer said he saw the airplane land, and two young men climbed out of the cockpit, but he doesn't know where they went.

A fisherman near Powell River caught a six-pound carp, and inside the fish's chest and belly were three three-foot-long snakes.

A dog in Southwest Africa was running near a political institution when it jumped forty feet straight up from the ground, landing perfectly on a parked car. The dog was unharmed, but the car was badly damaged.

A news article in the Perth newspaper says that there are 380,000,000 unmarried women in the world.

An old woman, Madame Bauer Jack, has lived in Juchislavl Lundlana for sixty years, and she is very poor. Recently, she received a letter from America, and she knew she had a few friends there. However, this letter was sent by an uncle she had forgotten about for a long time. He has now passed away and left her a legacy of $5,000,000. The news has been confirmed by the American consulate.

An old man with a beard, seventy-nine years old, living in Johannesburg, reached out to light a candle in bed. The head of the match bounced off his beard and immediately ignited. Sparks splashed onto the quilt, which also caught fire. The old man burned to death in bed.

A shoemaker living in Holton (Indiana) shot and killed two teachers at his son's school because his eleven-year-old son was disappointed with his grades. Then he killed himself.

Mrs. Barrett of Rosina has been married eleven times and divorced thirteen times in her lifetime. Now she has committed suicide with a gun. According to the police, her suicide was entirely due to love failure.

An old woman appeared in court in Venkovci, Juchislavl, seeking damages of a few pounds from a merchant because she had sold him eggs and butter. "How old are you?" the judge asked. The old woman replied, "If God allows, next year's birthday will be the 116th anniversary." Her name is Heihua Kovez. She has been widowed four times in her life. Although she is old, her spirit is still strong, and she can walk on her own.

American savings banks are now allowing people to deposit money using fingerprints instead of signatures. This is to prevent forged signatures. A twenty-pound piece of quartz fell on a miner's head while he was mining in California. In retaliation, he broke the stone with a hammer. To his surprise, there was a layer of pure gold on the surface worth 700 pounds.

A farmer in Sofia was very worried when his cow fell ill. One night, he heard a sound that he thought was the cow moaning, wanting something. He got up quickly, but soon after he left the bed, the cow was robbed by the guerrillas in gunfire.
